Furthermore, the eating must be with the intention for the sake of Heaven, as explained in Tanya that ordinary eating, which is not to fulfill his desire but only for the need of his sustenance, but is not for the sake of Heaven, is also *kelipas nogah* and needs refinement and ascent. Therefore, there must be a matter of war with oneself. This is not the case in Torah study, which is refinement through tranquility. And in Torah study itself, there are the two modes mentioned above (as explained above): in the *nigleh* (revealed) Torah, which is clothed in material things—these are kosher and these are *treif* —there must be a refinement of the permitted from the forbidden. Although it is refinement through tranquility, it only has the power to refine the permitted. Higher than this is the study of the *pnimiyus* (inner) Torah, which deals with spiritual matters, such as "He fills all worlds and surrounds all worlds," and the matter of the *Atzmus* (essence) of the *Ein Sof* with His revelations. This study effects the connection of the student to the *Atzmus* and essence of the *Ein Sof*, and from the connection to His *Atzmus*, "even darkness does not darken from You, and night shines like the day," and therefore the matter of war does not apply to this at all. An example of this is the eating and drinking of Shabbos, where the pleasure is a Divine pleasure, as it is written, "Then you shall delight in Hashem," for it is a mitzvah to delight in it with fatty meat and spiced wine, but the pleasure is a Divine pleasure. And so it is also with the eating and drinking that comes after engaging in the *pnimiyus* of Torah. This is what everyone can learn in their *avodah* from the revelation of Chanukah: that even during his business and eating and drinking, there is in it the matter of connection to the *Atzmus* and essence of the *Ein Sof*, from which "even darkness does not darken from You," etc., which is the matter of the Chanukah lamp—that the refinement is through tranquility, as it will be in the future.

## With the help of Hashem. Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Vayeishev, 23 Kislev, Mevarchim Chodesh Teves, 5712.

### With the help of Hashem. Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Vayeishev, 23 Kislev, Mevarchim Chodesh Teves, 5712.

(Unedited)

1. On this Shabbos, there are three matters: (a) Shabbos Parshas Vayeishev, (b) Shabbos Mevarchim Chodesh Teves, (c) the Shabbos before Chanukah, as it is known that the day of Shabbos relates to the days of the week that follow it.
In the Gemara we find that only the first three days belong to the Shabbos that preceded them, which are called "after Shabbos," while the three days that follow—from Wednesday onwards—already belong to the Shabbos that follows them, which are called "before Shabbos." However, in the discourses of Chassidus we find that all the days of the week belong to the Shabbos that preceded them, for "from it all the days are blessed". In any event, since Chanukah falls within the first three days following Shabbos, it certainly belongs to that Shabbos.
It is necessary to explain the connection and relevance of Chanukah to the parsha of the week that we read on the Shabbos preceding it—Parshas Vayeishev.

2. Maamar beginning "Vayeishev Yaakov, etc."

3. It is the custom of Yisrael that on the Shabbos before the wedding, the chassan is called to the Torah.
This is also hinted at in the text of the blessing practiced among Bnei Yisrael [and since the matter has been established in the custom of Yisrael, there is certainly a basis for it]: "To raise him to Torah, to chuppah, etc."—"to Torah" first, and afterwards "to chuppah." Therefore, before going to the chuppah (on the Shabbos before the wedding), the chassan is called to the Torah.
One must add an explanation regarding the continuation and conclusion of the blessing, "(to Torah and to chuppah) *and to good deeds*." Seemingly, this is difficult to understand: It is well and good that "to Torah (and afterwards) to chuppah" fits the general order of life: Torah—"from the time he begins to speak, his father teaches him Torah, etc.", and chuppah—"at eighteen years to chuppah". But "good deeds" precede chuppah, for when he is six years old, there is an obligation of "good deeds" (mitzvos) by Rabbinic law as a matter of chinuch, and when he is thirteen years old he becomes obligated in mitzvos by Torah law, and only after this does the time for entering the chuppah arrive. Moreover, before he becomes obligated in mitzvos ("good deeds"), entering the chuppah is not applicable, for the kiddushin of a minor are not valid kiddushin.

4. This can be explained by first clarifying what is written in our parsha, "And behold a vine was before me, and in the vine were three branches," and Chazal expounded, "The vine—this is Yisrael... the three branches—these are the three regalim."
It is known that the three regalim correspond to the three Avos: "The festivals were instituted corresponding to the Avos: Pesach corresponds to Avraham, as it is written, 'Knead and make cakes,' and it was Pesach; Shavuos corresponds to Yitzchak, for the sounding of the shofar of Matan Torah was with the shofar from the ram of Yitzchak; Sukkos corresponds to Yaakov, as it is written, 'And for his cattle he made sukkos'".
More specifically:
Pesach corresponds to Avraham—because the attribute of Avraham is the attribute of Chesed, and Pesach is connected with the concept of Chesed, as it is written, "And I will pass over (and have pity on) you, and there will be no plague upon you, etc.", "It is the Pesach sacrifice to Hashem, who passed over the houses of Bnei Yisrael in Mitzrayim when He struck Mitzrayim and saved our houses".
Shavuos corresponds to Yitzchak—because the attribute of Yitzchak is the attribute of Gevurah, and on Shavuos the Torah was given from the mouth of the Gevurah, with "thunder and lightning, etc., and the voice of the shofar was very strong, and all the people trembled", "And the people saw and moved (from the word for trembling and quaking) and stood from afar".
One should add to the explanation of Yitzchak’s connection to Shavuos—regarding the matter of marriage—that the wedding of Yitzchak is the first wedding that the Torah describes at length, "doubled in the Torah" [as Chazal said, "The conversation of the servants of the Avos is more beautiful than the Torah of the children, for the parsha of Eliezer (the shidduch of Yitzchak and Rivkah) is doubled in the Torah, and many bodies of Torah were given only by hint"], because the marriage of Yitzchak and Rivkah is the general concept of the yichud of Ze’er Anpin and Nukvah (Mah and Ban), which is the general avodah of Bnei Yisrael, and this is also the concept of the festival of Shavuos, Matan Torah, the marriage and yichud of Knesses Yisrael with the Ribono shel Olam.
Sukkos corresponds to Yaakov—because Yaakov’s essence is Torah, as it is written, "And Yaakov was a simple man, dwelling in tents," "the tent of Shem and the tent of Ever" [and as the Tzemach Tzedek explains that "(the tent of) Shem refers to the Written Torah, similar to how the entire Torah is the names of the Ribono shel Olam... and (the tent of) Ever refers to the Oral Torah... similar to 'and he passes before them'... 'passing' means the drawing down below, and the drawing down and revelation of the Torah is through the Oral Torah"], and Sukkos is connected with the concept of Torah, "for it is written, 'And I have put My words in your mouth and in the shadow of My hand I have covered you,' for from the Torah a state of Sukkah, a state of shadow, is created, for the breath of speech becomes an Ohr Makif for the neshamah", and as explained in Tanya that "the Torah is food and also a garment for the intellectual soul which is clothed in it through its contemplation and study, and all the more so when he utters it with his mouth in speech, for the breath of speech becomes a state of Ohr Makif."
This is the concept of "The vine—this is Yisrael... the three branches—these are the three regalim" in the avodah of man—that in every one of Yisrael, who are called a "vine," there must be "three branches," the concepts of the three regalim that correspond to Avraham, Yitzchak, and Yaakov.
- In addition to the fact that they themselves are called "branches," as Chazal expounded, "The vine is the world, the three branches are Avraham, Yitzchak, and Yaakov"—who are called "Avos," "We only call the three of them Avos", as is known the difference between the Avos and the Shevatim, that "the Shevatim... there is a person who does not have these levels and qualities at all, whereas the quality of the Avos must be in every person", meaning that in every one of Yisrael ("vine") there must be the concepts of the three Avos ("three branches")—that his avodah must be both in the line of Chesed (the attribute of Avraham) and in the line of Gevurah (the attribute of Yitzchak), and this matter is accomplished through the Torah (the concept of Yaakov), from the word *hora'ah* (instruction), through which one knows when to use the line of Chesed and when to use the line of Gevurah.

5. The explanation of this matter in the avodah of man:
From the side of the attribute of Chesed (the attribute of Avraham) by itself, the influence of Chesed can be in an undesirable manner, such as to one who is not worthy. Therefore, there must also be the attribute of Gevurah (the attribute of Yitzchak), so that through this, the influence of Chesed will also be in the appropriate manner.
As we find with Avraham Avinu himself, that even though his attribute was the attribute of Chesed, nevertheless, he also possessed the attribute of Gevurah—as it is written, "Now I know that you are a *G-d-fearing man*," for yirah is the attribute of Gevurah, and it is written, "For I have known him, so that he may command his children, etc., to do tzedakah *and justice*," not only tzedakah (Chesed), but also justice (Gevurah).

6. For example, in the daily life of every one of Yisrael:
From the side of the attribute of Chesed by itself—he can engage in acts of kindness also (and on the contrary: first and foremost) *with himself*... for even though he does not deceive himself and knows his standing and situation that he is not worthy, he argues that the essence of the attribute of Chesed is to perform kindness even for one who is not worthy, and all the more so *a fortiori* from Avraham Avinu, who performed kindness even for Arabs, and certainly when it concerns a son of Avraham, Yitzchak, and Yaakov, he must certainly perform kindness with him.
Consequently, when it concerns davening with a minyan, setting times for Torah, and the like—he argues that first and foremost he must perform kindness with himself. And since it is currently cold or hot outside, and he has not yet slept enough, he will postpone the davening or the engagement in Torah to a later time, according to his convenience—after a pleasant sleep, and furthermore—he argues—that according to the Rambam, the time for sleep is "eight hours," and as the Magen Avraham brings a sign for this from what is written, "I slept, *then* (in gematria eight) I have rest," and only then will his head be calm and free for the study of Torah.
And similarly when it concerns a matter of iskafya, for example—as brought in Tanya—"that he desires to eat and delays his meal until an hour or less later and engages in Torah during that hour, as it is in the Gemara, the fourth hour is the mealtime for all people, the sixth hour is the mealtime for a talmid chacham, and they would starve themselves for two hours for this intention, even though they would study all day even after the meal":
Being a "lamdan" and also a "chossid"—he knows what is written in Iggeres HaTeshuvah regarding the matter of fasts and self-afflictions, that "even in the early generations... they would not fast in such a manner except for the healthy," whereas "who... is it possible that he could come to illness or pain, G-d forbid, as in these generations of ours," and consequently—he argues—he cannot delay his meal, but he must be careful every day to eat breakfast *at its time*!
If he were in the time of the Shas, and one of the "healthy"—he argues—he could delay breakfast for two hours: despite the fact that the custom in all of America is to eat "breakfast" at the set time, he would perform iskafya *after* the "breakfast" by "two hours."
To abolish the custom—"G-d forbid"... but to delay—he would be able to.
Whereas "in these generations of ours," when we are not like the "healthy" ones of the time of the Shas—how can he delay his meal?!...
And in general—he continues to argue—"a person is not permitted to injure himself" [and there are even opinions that one who injures himself transgresses a negative commandment, as it is said, "Only guard yourself and guard your soul very much," "and wherever it is said 'guard,' it is nothing but a negative commandment"], and even though "one who injures himself... is exempt," nevertheless, "he is not permitted" [meaning, he is exempt from human courts but liable in the courts of Heaven], "because a person’s soul is not his possession (like his property, over which he is the owner), but the possession of the Ribono shel Olam", and therefore, "a person has no authority over his body at all... *and not to cause it any pain, even by withholding some food and drink*" (as written by our Rebbe HaZaken), and consequently, how can he—he argues—cause himself pain by delaying the meal?!...
- The truth is that our Rebbe HaZaken clarifies this in the continuation of his words: "Unless he does so by way of teshuvah, for this pain is good for him to save his soul, etc.". -
And furthermore—he argues—not only is it forbidden to cause himself pain, but on the contrary, he must *perform kindness with himself*... as mentioned, that he must conduct himself according to the attribute of Avraham, the attribute of Chesed.
Therefore, one must also emphasize the need for the attribute of *Gevurah*, meaning, that regarding himself one should not be particular about conducting oneself with the attribute of Chesed, but must conduct oneself (also) with the attribute of Gevurah.
As explained in the discourses of Chassidus in the interpretation of the verse, "Justice and tzedakah in Yaakov You have done," "justice" (Gevurah) and afterwards "tzedakah" (Chesed), that in order for the giving of tzedakah to others to be as it should be, one must first judge oneself as to how much is fitting for him, and the rest he should give to tzedakah, meaning, that regarding himself there must be a matter of justice, specifically through the attribute of Gevurah.

7. However, although generally there should be conduct of Chesed (tzedakah) regarding others, and conduct of Gevurah (justice) regarding oneself, nevertheless, we find sometimes that even regarding oneself the attribute of Gevurah is negated.
For example: it is known the saying of the Baal Shem Tov in the interpretation of the verse, "If you see the donkey of your enemy, etc., you shall surely help with him," that "donkey" refers to the materiality of the body, which is "your enemy," that it hates the neshamah, etc., and nevertheless, one should not break the materiality of the body, but rather, clarify and refine it.
From this it is understood that even regarding oneself, one must sometimes be careful that there should not be conduct of Gevurah (breaking the materiality of the body), but rather conduct of Chesed (clarification and refinement), although the Chesed must be in the correct measure and in the desired manner.

8. And similarly in other matters of this kind—for example: sometimes one must conduct oneself in a manner of expansion and elevation (the line of Chesed), as Chazal said, "A talmid chacham must have in him one-eighth of an eighth," but one must be careful that it be in the appropriate manner.
- Once, someone complained in his letter to the Rebbe Rayatz that in the shul where he davens, they dismiss him because he has in him the "eighth of an eighth" [it was, apparently, a Chassidic shul, or even a popular one, where they noticed his conduct that he is "metahader" in the "eighth of an eighth"].
It does not bother him—he continues in his letter—the fact *that they dismiss him*, for in knowing his standing and situation, he knows that he deserves it; it only bothers him that his presence causes those standing around him (who dismiss him) to act in an undesirable manner [for they do not know his calculations that he deserves it, etc., and their intention is merely *simply* to dismiss him], and in doing so *they stumble him* in... "before the blind you shall not place a stumbling block"!...
He does—he continues in his letter—what he can to prevent them; he tells them not to dismiss him so that they will not stumble in "before the blind," but to no avail, they do not listen to him. And therefore—he concludes in his letter—he requests the Rebbe’s advice on what to do so as not to cause Yidden to act in an undesirable manner...
The Rebbe replied to him that the truth is indeed that "a talmid chacham must have in him one-eighth of an eighth," but the approach to this can be from two ends:
One can start from the end of "talmid chacham," strive to be a talmid chacham, and only afterwards add also the "eighth of an eighth," and one can start immediately from the end of "eighth of an eighth." And the practical difference is also in the relationship to him from above—that when one starts immediately from the "eighth of an eighth," he arouses extra attention among the angels [and all the more so that as they always stand in a state of bitul, they feel and notice immediately when they see an opposite movement], and they immediately pounce on him to measure the "talmid chacham" in him, whether it is indeed in accordance with the "eighth of an eighth" in him...
It is better, therefore—the Rebbe concludes in his letter—not to start with the end of "eighth of an eighth," but to start from the end of "talmid chacham."

9. And here the correct distinction in the matter is required—to be careful not to exchange and mix between Chesed and Gevurah, not to use this attribute at a time when one should use its opposite, but to know in which matter one should conduct oneself in the line of Chesed and in which matter one should conduct oneself in the line of Gevurah.
And this distinction is made by *Torah* (the concept of Yaakov), from the word *hora'ah*, which teaches and shows the correct path in every matter, and through it, conduct in the two lines of Chesed (the attribute of Avraham) and Gevurah (the attribute of Yitzchak) is accomplished as it should be.

10. Based on this, one must also explain the order of "Torah, chuppah, and good deeds":
"Good deeds"—which refers to the performance of mitzvos—in general, the mitzvah of *tzedakah* [which "is called by the name mitzvah simply... because it is the essence of the practical mitzvos and surpasses them all... there is no mitzvah in which the vital soul is clothed as much as the mitzvah of tzedakah... all the power of his vital soul is clothed in the performance of his work or other business from which he earned these coins, and when he gives them to tzedakah, all the power of his vital soul ascends to Hashem", and furthermore that "(even though he gives only a fifth, the) fifth elevates with it all the other four parts to Hashem"], which comes from the side of the attribute of *Chesed*.
And "chuppah"—the concept of marriage which is connected with the festival of Shavuos that corresponds to Yitzchak (as above, section 4)—belongs to and hints at the attribute of *Gevurah*, which is the introduction to "good deeds," as mentioned above (section 6) that there is a need for the introduction of the line of Gevurah so that the influence from the side of the attribute of Chesed will be in the appropriate manner. And the introduction to all this is the concept of "Torah," as mentioned, through which one knows how the conduct in the two lines of Chesed and Gevurah should be.

11. One should add to this—regarding marriage in its literal sense:
Good deeds—from the side of the line of Chesed—indicates the influence of the chassan to the kallah in the matter of marriage.
And in order for this influence to be in the appropriate manner, there must first be an entry into the chuppah, "that he brings her into his house and yichuds with her and sets her apart for himself", which is the concept of limitation, the line of Gevurah.
And all this is done according to the instruction of the Torah, through which one knows how the limitation (Gevurah) and the influence (Chesed) should be in the appropriate manner.
And another matter in this—that the introduction of "Torah" hints at the aliyah to the Torah on the Shabbos before the marriage (as above, section 3), which is an *is'arusa d'leila* and the giving of strength to the general matter of marriage: "chuppah"—whose purpose is yichud and procreation, "be fruitful and multiply", both in physicality and in spirituality, and "good deeds"—the going out to engage in the matters of the world in a manner of "all your deeds shall be for the sake of Heaven" and "in all your ways know Him", which, the "*deeds*" ("all your deeds" in the clarification of the world) are specifically "*good*," "good and illuminating", like precious stones and pearls, stones specifically, which are specifically set in the king’s crown, and they are the ones that illuminate the king’s crown.

12. Here is the place to add and complete in continuation of what was discussed at the farbrengen of 19 Kislev regarding the decree that existed in its time against the study of Chassidus, that since the reason for the decree was explained at the time, then when the reason is nullified, the decree is nullified:
They asked me: Where is the source for this law, that a decree whose reason was explained, when the reason is nullified, the decree is nullified?
And the answer to this:
It is in the Gemara, "When they decree a decree in the West, they do not reveal the reason until twelve months, lest there be a person who does not agree with it (who does not have that reason and does not separate from it) and comes to treat it lightly," "but now that they do not reveal the reason for the matter, everyone separates from it, for they think that the Rabbis stood on a matter from which ruin comes, and it is we who are not expert in the reason" (whereas after twelve months, when the prohibition is already known and publicized and established, they announce the reason).
And in the continuation of the sugya we find that "they do not decree a decree upon the public unless the majority of the public can stand by it," in which is also included that the majority of the public *accepts* this decree, whereas when "the people were doubtful about it and it did not spread to the majority of the community, it is nullified" (meaning, that it is nullified even for the minority that accepted it).
And since this is so, when they decree a decree and reveal the reason immediately (before the prohibition is known and established)—this can be in one of two ways: (a) because of the *certainty* that this decree will be accepted and spread among the public without objection, so that there is no concern that perhaps there is a person who does not have that reason, (b) *on the contrary*: because of the *doubt* that the Sages doubted whether the decree would be accepted by the majority of the public or if the people would be doubtful about it, therefore, they publicized the reason for the decree from the outset, in order to test if the reason for the decree is accepted by the majority of the public, so that the decree is from the outset on the doubt. And from this we come to the distinction mentioned between the two ways in decrees:
When a Beis Din decrees a decree and does not reveal the reason (until the prohibition is known and established), so that those for whom the reason for the decree is not accepted will not come to treat the decree lightly—since it is the will of the Beis Din that the decree be in its existence in all ways, even if there are those for whom the reason is not accepted (which is why they did not reveal the reason), then even when the reason is nullified, the decree remains in its existence, and it cannot be nullified except by a Beis Din that is greater than it.
But when a Beis Din decrees a decree and reveals the reason immediately, and after some time it is clarified that the reason is nullified—since from the outset they revealed the reason because of the *doubt* if the reason for the decree would be accepted, then when the reason is nullified, the decree is nullified, because from the outset the decree was *on the doubt*, and in such a manner (when the reason is nullified) they did not decree from the outset.
And similarly regarding our matter—the decree that the *misnagdim* decreed upon Chassidim:
And by way of introduction, the saying of the Rebbe Rayatz regarding the names "Chassidim" and "Misnagdim," that seemingly the Misnagdim should have called the Chassidim by the name Misnagdim, for the Baal Shem Tov and those who follow him are the ones who *innovated* a new method *in opposition* to the old way that was accepted until then, whereas the others only continued in the old way as before, so that seemingly they were not worthy to be called by the name Misnagdim, since their matter is not opposition, a matter of negation? — Rather, it was established by *Divine Providence* that they themselves called those who follow the method of the Baal Shem Tov by the name "Chassidim," a matter of affirmation, and themselves they called by the name "Misnagdim," a matter of negation, which has no repair and building, but destruction and ruin.
And accordingly, it was also established by Divine Providence that the decree they decreed upon Chassidim was in a manner that from the outset they revealed the reason for the decree, without waiting twelve months (when the decree remains in its existence even after the reason is nullified), meaning, that the existence of the decree depends on the reason, and consequently, when the reason is nullified, the decree is nullified.

13. Furthermore:
The existence of a decree (even when they did not reveal the reason for the decree immediately) also depends on it being in all of Yisrael, all Bnei Yisrael, and in the entire world, whereas a decree that is only in certain places (and types), and there are places (and types) where they did not decree (because the reason for the decree does not apply to them), then when the reason is nullified, the decree is nullified, like in the places where they did not decree from the outset.
And in our case—the decree on the method of Chassidus was not in all countries, and not upon all Bnei Yisrael, but only upon a part, for in the countries of the East the decree did not exist, and also not upon those who reached the age of forty; the decree was only upon those who did not reach the age of forty, and only in certain countries, where they feared new things that might cause undesirable results, as had already occurred in these countries in the generation before.
And since this is so, when the reason is nullified, the decree is nullified even in those places where the decree existed, which become like those places where the decree was not decreed from the outset.

14. And another essential matter in all the above:
What was said above regarding the two reasons for the nullification of the decree (that they revealed the reason for the decree immediately, and from the outset it was not in all places) is even when *at the time* the reason for the decree was *true* (at that time and in that place where the decree was decreed), but *after some time* the reason was nullified.
But in our case—from the outset (at the time of the decree) *the reason was not true*, and consequently, the decree was *by mistake*, so certainly when one sees that the reason is not true, there is no place for this decree that was by mistake from the outset.
And one should not wonder how it is possible that so-and-so, a great man in Yisrael, would make a mistake?!—for we find in many places in the Shas about the great Amoraim who declared, "The things I told you are a mistake on my part," and similarly we find even with Moshe Rabbeinu that "whoever comes to anger comes to mistake".

15. The truth is that those great men of Yisrael who went out against the Torah of Chassidus had some connection ("*eppes a sheichus*") to the Torah of Chassidus, and because of this they had the "breadth" and the strength to oppose the Torah of Chassidus, whereas if they had no connection at all to the Torah of Chassidus, they would not have had the strength to oppose the Torah of Chassidus.
- At one of the farbrengens that took place in Warsaw in the year 5698, when the Chossid R' Yitzchak HaMatmid took mashke a little more than the measure (the Rebbe smiled and said: "He didn't need much"...), he said to the Rebbe Rayatz that according to his opinion ("*vi bei im leigt zich*") the title "*knows his Master* and (nevertheless it is possible that) intends to rebel against Him" is appropriate—specifically for a Chossid, for about a goy—it is certainly not applicable to say "knows his Master," and to distinguish, about a Misnagid—it is also not applicable to say in truth "knows his Master," for in what is his knowledge expressed ("*vos veist er*")?!... "Knows his Master" is applicable to say, therefore, only about a Chossid who studies the Torah of Chassidus, and the more he adds in the study of the Torah of Chassidus, the more his knowledge of his Master grows ("*vos mer Chossid is alts mer yodea*").
The Rebbe Rayatz did not accept and nullified this saying (because for a true Chossid who knows his Master, the matter of intending to rebel against Him is certainly not applicable), but the idea itself (that only about the "knower" is it applicable to say that he "intends to rebel against Him") has a place in the intellect. And similarly in our case, that the opposition to the Torah of Chassidus could only be by someone who had some connection to the Torah of Chassidus.
And similar to what is explained in Toras Shalom that the betrayal was by a *Yid* specifically, who also has a "reflection of a reflection" of the *Yechidah* in the soul (unlike the nations of the world who "have a reflection, but do not have a reflection of a reflection"), and therefore through him specifically can there be opposition to the Torah of Chassidus, whose matter is the revelation of the level of *Yechidah*, because, even to be an opponent one needs some connection.
However, even he who has a "reflection of a reflection" (Yechidah in the soul) could not bring his opposition into action (to succeed in it), G-d forbid, because the Torah of Chassidus is above even the level of Yechidah [as is known that even the level of Yechidah is only a name, as the precision of the language "five *names* are called for it, nefesh, ruach, neshamah, chayah, *yechidah*"], and reaches *the Essence itself* ("Chassidus derlangt

## Sicha, Night of the Fifth Candle of Chanukah, 5712

### - To the students of Yeshivas Tomchei Temimim in Brooklyn -

**1.** The holy Nasi’im, the Admorim—the founders and directors of Yeshivas Tomchei Temimim Lubavitch—had a profound desire and will that the students of the Yeshiva be *“candles to illuminate.”* They did not want them to be merely students, not just students who study, and not just Chassidim in the abstract, but rather *“candles to illuminate,”* as they precisely phrased it.

The precision of the language—*“candles”* and also *“to illuminate”*—is significant. It is possible for a candle to be complete in its components: the wick, the oil, and the vessel, yet the essential element—*“to illuminate”*—is missing. The candle is not burning; it is not illuminating for itself or for others.

It is therefore imperative that they be *“candles to illuminate”*: to find the candle within one’s own soul, with all its parts, and to ignite it. The description is specifically *“to illuminate”*—not just “a light” or “an illuminator”—which means that the *purpose* of the candles is specifically to illuminate. Furthermore, it can be said that this includes another matter: one is required and obligated to ignite the candle of one’s fellow as well—that is, to find the oil, the wick, and the vessel within one’s fellow, and to ignite it.

**2.** Since this is the intention of the founders and directors of the Yeshiva, we may deduce two things: First, that they invested their holy powers into this. Second, that through this, the ability and the powers to bring all of this into actuality have been created within you (even if they were not there initially).

However, Hashem does not want it to be in the manner of "bread of shame." As our Rebbe related, the Alter Rebbe, his son the Mitteler Rebbe, and his grandson the Tzemach Tzedek labored with great exertion so that the Chassidim would be G-d-fearing through their own *avodah*, and not G-d-fearing as a matter of course. Therefore, trials and obstacles arise for every individual that prevent him from bringing this into actuality. Sometimes it seems to him that it is an obstacle, and sometimes it is a genuine obstacle. To one, it seems that such *avodah* is too heavy; to another, it seems that he is already in the state of a “candle to illuminate” because he is already suited to the intention—even though, in practice, he is still very far from it.

But since this is the will of a Rebbe and a Tzaddik, and our Sages have said that “Tzaddikim are similar to their Creator,” just as with Hashem, “He devises thoughts so that no one is cast away from Him,” so too, the Nasi and Rebbe—who is the intermediary connecting the Jewish people in general, and his students and those connected to him in particular, with Hashem—also possesses this quality, that no one is cast away from him. And come what may, it must come into actuality. As the Alter Rebbe says in Tanya and Shulchan Aruch, Hilchos Talmud Torah, “in this incarnation or in another incarnation.”

“In another incarnation” does not necessarily mean after one hundred and twenty years, when the soul departs from the body. That is the simple interpretation. But “another incarnation” also includes when a fundamental change occurs within a person during his lifetime in this world.

And since one can do and rectify this one hour sooner, why and for what reason should one postpone the rectification to a later time? It is a well-known saying of the Rebbe Rayatz in his sichos: Chassidim are clever; they do not postpone until tomorrow what can be done today. “Today to perform them.”

**3.** On the nights of Chanukah, we light the Chanukah candles, and this matter (the lighting of the Chanukah candles) is the *essence* of the mitzvah of Chanukah.

Both women and men are obligated to light. The candles of Shabbos and Yom Tov were entrusted to women, but Chanukah candles are for both men—who are accustomed to conquering—and women, meaning even one who thinks he does not have, or truly does not have, the strength and the power of expansion to conquer and influence others. Yet, even he can illuminate his own environment, at the very least.

The time for lighting them is “from when the sun sets,” when darkness descends upon the world, when all the oils (even those) in the Sanctuary were defiled, and even after the destruction of the Beis Hamikdash.

This means that Chanukah candles are to illuminate the darkness of the *sitra achra*, the darkness of the exile.

“It is a mitzvah to place the Chanukah candle at the entrance of his house from the outside”—the Jewish people in general, and the students of the Yeshivos in particular, must ensure that even their “external” matters are illuminated with the light of Torah and the true intention.

“It is a mitzvah to place it... on the left”—not only to illuminate his right side, but also his left side. And even more: to illuminate the *other*, even one who is truly an “other”—to find the candle within him as well and to ignite it.

“On the first day one lights one, and from then on, one adds and continues”—every night an additional candle illuminates—“a mitzvah is a candle, and Torah is light.”

In what manner and through what is it certain that this will be so? “Look at the end of the verse”: “(A mitzvah is a candle, and Torah is light) and the way of life is the rebuke of instruction”—the “way of life” is the connection to the Luminary of the Torah, the inner dimension of the Torah, which in the recent generations has been explained in the teachings of Chassidus—as explained at length in the *Kuntres Eitz Chaim*.

May Hashem help that you, the *“candles to illuminate,”* fulfill the intention of the founders and directors of the Yeshiva, and may it be with kindness and mercy, and may they receive *nachas* from you—Chassidishe *nachas*—in every detail.

**4.** Today is Chanukah. And the custom of Israel is Torah, to distribute *Chanukah gelt*. I will give you *Chanukah gelt* from the fund of the Rebbe Rayatz. According to the words of our Rebbe, it is similar to the saying of our Sages, “He who takes a *prutah* from Iyov is blessed.” It does not concern the amount—even the smallest coin, even a *prutah*—but since this *prutah* is from a “man... perfect and upright, G-d-fearing and shunning evil,” the one who takes it is blessed, and he will have success. And when our Rebbe says “success,” he means not only in material matters, but also in spiritual ones.

[The Rebbe handed out *Chanukah gelt*—one silver shekel—to each of the students who entered.]

*Chanukah tova* (“A gut Chanukah”).

## Sicha of the Night of the Fifth Candle of Chanukah, 5712

**A. Every event in the world occurs through Divine Providence, and this is especially true of events in the lives of Bnei Yisrael. In the most specific of details—the marriage of a Jewish man and woman—since its purpose is to be an "everlasting edifice" for generations to come, it is certainly a matter of Divine Providence.**

**In accordance with this, we must explain the connection and relevance between the content of a wedding and the time in which it occurs, which, by Divine Providence, is during the days of Chanukah (on the fifth candle), as follows.**

**B. Regarding the mitzvah of lighting the Chanukah candles, we find that women are also obligated, as it is stated in the Gemara: "Women are obligated in the mitzvah of Chanukah, for they too were part of that miracle."**

**There are two details to this:**
**1) "The decree was very much against them," and in the words of the Rambam, "They laid their hands on their property and on their daughters," as explained in Rashi’s commentary.**
**2) The salvation from the decree was brought about by a woman.**

**Because of this—even though the mitzvah of Chanukah is a time-bound positive commandment, and women are generally exempt from all time-bound positive commandments—women are nonetheless obligated in it. (It is well known what the Rebbe Rayatz related: that his father, the Rebbe Rashab, when he had to leave his home during the days of Chanukah, would instruct his wife, the Rebbetzin Sterna Sarah, that even though she would hear the blessing of lighting the Chanukah candles from one of the men, she should nevertheless light the Chanukah candles herself.)**

**C. We must explain the reason for this (in the inner dimension of things): why the salvation of the Chanukah miracle came about specifically through a woman (which is why they are obligated, as mentioned, in lighting the Chanukah candle).**

**By way of introduction: on the face of it, the reason for the mesiras nefesh of Bnei Yisrael in their war against the Greeks is difficult to understand. After all, according to the letter of the law, Bnei Yisrael were seemingly not obligated in this mesiras nefesh, because the decree of the Greeks was regarding mitzvos for which the law is "transgress and do not be killed."**

**Furthermore, regarding the decree on "their daughters" (as mentioned in section 2), while the prohibition of forbidden relations is indeed in the category of "be killed and do not transgress," there is the argument that "Esther was like soil," and furthermore, it is implied from Rashi’s words that this decree was for the sake of "their own pleasure," and consequently, the argument that "their own pleasure is different" would also apply.**

**Even though the aforementioned distinction between other mitzvos (where one should "transgress and not be killed") and the three cardinal sins (where one should "be killed and not transgress") applies only when it is not a time of religious persecution—"but in a time of religious persecution, when an evil king stands... and decrees persecution upon Israel to nullify their religion or any of the mitzvos, one must be killed and not transgress, even for one of the other mitzvos"—and it appears simple that the situation during the days of Chanukah was a time of decree, there is still room for inquiry as to whether Bnei Yisrael were obligated to go out to war over this. For the meaning of the rule that in a time of persecution one must be "killed and not transgress" for all mitzvos is that if a person is forced to transgress the mitzvos of the Torah, he must sacrifice his life rather than transgress; but seemingly, one cannot say there is an obligation to *go out to war* against an evil kingdom to nullify decrees.**

**According to this, an explanation is required: why did Bnei Yisrael go out to war against the Greeks and sacrifice their lives in a manner they were not at all obligated to do?**

**The explanation for this is as follows:**
**Since the decree during the days of Chanukah involved matters connected to the "purity of the family" (as mentioned in section 2)—a matter that touches upon the *holiness of the nation*, upon which the holiness of the Jewish people for *all* future generations also depends—Bnei Yisrael did not consider at all whether or not they were obligated to sacrifice their lives. Instead, they went out to war with a mesiras nefesh that was *above reason and intellect*, in order to guard and ensure the holiness of the nation.**

**This was brought about by the *Jewish women* of that generation:**
**In addition to the fact that the women themselves fought against the Greeks with the same mesiras nefesh that they were not obligated to have (just like the men), the Jewish women *were the ones who aroused and influenced* the men to awaken with a feeling of mesiras nefesh *above* reason and intellect, and to go out to open war against the Greeks.**

**And since the special avodah of mesiras nefesh that we find in the days of Chanukah (mesiras nefesh above reason and intellect) was awakened and brought about by the Jewish women, they merited that the *miracle of salvation* also came about *specifically through them*.**

**D. It can be said that since Hashem rewards Israel "measure for measure," therefore, just as the avodah of the Jewish people at that time was in a manner of mesiras nefesh above reason and intellect, the reward given to them by Hashem (for their avodah) was also above reason and intellect.**

**There are two details to this: (1) The victory in the war against the Greeks—which was a victory *above nature*. (2) The miracle of finding the pure cruse of oil, and furthermore, the primary miracle that followed—that even though "it contained only enough to light for one day, a miracle occurred and they lit from it for eight days."**

**This can be understood by way of introduction: seemingly, why did the Jewish people need the miracle of finding the pure cruse of oil, and for the oil to burn for eight days? After all, "impurity is permitted (or set aside) in a communal setting," and if so, they would have been permitted to light the Menorah even with impure oil?**

**The explanation for this is that Hashem rewarded Bnei Yisrael "measure for measure." Since *they* sacrificed their lives in a manner *beyond and above* the letter of the law, Hashem rewarded them with the miracle of finding the cruse of pure oil and its burning for eight days, which is also above the letter of the law (since, according to the law, they were permitted, as mentioned, to light with impure oil).**

**E. From this, there is a lesson and instruction for every single Jew:**
**Regarding matters concerning the holiness of the nation, one should not do only what is permitted (and required) by the letter of the law. Rather, one must strengthen oneself and strive to act with mesiras nefesh above reason and intellect, *beyond* the letter of the law. Hashem then rewards measure for measure, so that even in matters where one could suffice with a certain measure, Hashem gives in great abundance—in material matters, and likewise in spiritual matters.**

**From here, there is also a lesson and instruction regarding the matter of marriage—for the building of a home in Israel ("an everlasting edifice"), on the foundation of purity and holiness, touches upon the *holiness of the nation*.**

**To add: just as in the days of Chanukah this was brought about especially through the avodah of mesiras nefesh of the Jewish women (as mentioned in section 3), so it is throughout all generations. As is evident, the primary conduct of the home (and everything related to it) is determined specifically by the *woman*.**

## B's"d. 4 Teves, 5712.

### B's"d. 4 Teves, 5712.

#### — To the committee organizing the chazarah of Chassidus, in the Rebbe’s study —

*Unedited*

[The Rebbe expressed his dissatisfaction with the report regarding the chazarah of Chassidus in the shuls on Shabbos, noting that the list of students participating in the chazarah was missing the names of many students, and said:]

You must convey to the students who are negligent and are not going to repeat Chassidus in the shuls—and if they wish, you may convey this in my name—that regardless of all the "excuses" (that it is difficult, that there are not enough shuls, and the like), this conduct is not the proper order at all, and they must go and repeat Chassidus in the shuls.

I would add and emphasize that this matter is (not only for the benefit of the baalei-batim who are in the shuls, but) also for the benefit of *the students themselves*.

Therefore, even if it is impossible to arrange for all the students to repeat Chassidus in the shuls because the number of shuls in the vicinity is insufficient for all the students, it is worthwhile and appropriate that they repeat Chassidus in "770," or in the yeshiva building ("in dem building"), before a group of students (and it is best if it is before nine students, so that there are ten together, in accordance with the statement of our Sages that "any matter of holiness must not be less than ten"), so that throughout the day of Shabbos, *all the students* will be able to repeat Chassidus.

(The Rebbe smiled and said:) And if one might argue based on the laws of eiruv that one must leave a "remainder" (shiur) so that the law of the public domain is not forgotten—a small remainder is sufficient... and apart from this small remainder, all the students must repeat Chassidus.

Likewise, you must urge the students who are going to repeat Chassidus in the shuls not to repeat difficult maamarim (as I have heard that some of them are repeating difficult maamarim), but rather easy maamarim, which are easier to explain through human intellect, especially since they must explain them in a way that will be grasped by the intellect of the baalei-batim who daven in the shuls where the maamarim are being repeated.

Accordingly, it is worthwhile and appropriate that the mashpiim in charge of the study of Chassidus instruct the students as to which maamarim are more suitable for repetition in shuls, and also examine the manner in which the students repeat the maamarim (as I have already requested to be conveyed to them).

In any case, the aforementioned details are secondary to the primary matter for which you were called—to arouse [the students] regarding the necessity and urgency of acting and influencing each and every student to go and repeat Chassidus, as stated above.

(The Rebbe concluded:)

"And if you will want to, you will succeed and accomplish."

## Sicha of Tuesday, Parshas Va’era, 24 Teves, 5712

**Unedited**

**1.** [The Rebbe instructed that a *Shas* distribution be organized using the designated cards. After the cards were distributed and the *Shas* was divided, the Rebbe said:]

The Rebbe Maharash writes in one of his *maamarim* that "at the very least, everyone should study one tractate of Gemara per year."

This study can be done individually, but it can also be done *together with the entire community*. In that case, the benefit is twofold: each of the learners takes a share in the study of the others and gives a share of his own study to all the others. Both the individual and the community benefit from this.

Consequently, everyone can join the *Shas* distribution currently being organized, where each person studies the tractate he accepts upon himself—in conjunction with the entire public—and the merit of the many assists them.

[The Rebbe instructed to announce that those who had not yet participated in the *Shas* distribution should do so now.]

**2.** (After Rabbi Meir Ashkenazi finished the *Shas*, the Rebbe said:) It is customary to conduct a *Hadran* on the *Shas*.

The *Hadran* on the *Shas*—an explanation of the dispute between Beis Shammai and Beis Hillel regarding whether a woman who sees blood on the eleventh day is considered to be observing a day by Torah law, and the dispute between Rabbi Yochanan and Reish Lakish (regarding the view of Beis Hillel) as to whether she is obligated to observe a day if she sees blood on the tenth—is based on their respective positions regarding whether a "half-measure" (*chatzi shiur*) is prohibited by Torah law. The dispute between Beis Shammai and Beis Hillel is based on their respective positions regarding whether we follow the "potential" (*b'koach*) or the "actual" (*b'po'al*). [This was edited by the Rebbe and printed in *Likkutei Sichos*, Vol. 7, p. 107 and onwards.]

At the conclusion of the *Hadran*—in connection with the *halacha* following Beis Hillel that we follow the "actual"—the Rebbe said:

One should connect this concept (that the *halacha* follows Beis Hillel, that "potential" is insufficient and it must be "actual") with the conclusion of the *Shas* in the Gemara: "The students of the school of Eliyahu taught: Whoever studies *halachos* every day is guaranteed to be a son of the World to Come, as it is said: 'His are the *halichos* (ways) of the world'; do not read *halichos* but *halachos*."

When a Jew knows that he must conduct himself in a manner of "His are the *halichos* of the world"—to study *halachos* constantly—and he generally conducts himself this way, then even if, due to circumstances beyond his control, a specific day passes in which he did not study *halachos* in actuality, this day is included in the day (and days) preceding it. Since at that time he stood, like every Jew who observes Torah and *mitzvos*, in a state of preparation to study the next day as well (if it were only within his ability), it turns out that the day preceding it already contains the "*potential*" for the study of this day as well, only that due to a cause not his fault, it did not come into actuality.

However, since the *halacha* follows Beis Hillel that it must be "actual" specifically, and also in laws pertaining to the matter of time, as in the examples explained in the *Hadran*—therefore, "The students of the school of Eliyahu taught: Whoever studies *halachos* *every day* (specifically) is guaranteed, etc.," meaning that the study of *halachos* must be *every day in actuality*. If the "actual" study of *halachos* is missing on a certain day, the "potential" of the previous day—that it was in his thought and mind to study the next day—does not suffice.

It is true that he will not be punished for not having studied *halachos* on that day because he was coerced, and "the Merciful One exempts one who is coerced," but nevertheless, we do not say "it is considered as if he did it." Since the "actual" study of *halachos* is missing for him on that day, the consequence that comes through it—"he is guaranteed, etc."—is also missing.

Only "whoever studies *halachos* every day" in actuality, then "he is guaranteed to be a son of the World to Come, as it is said: 'His are the *halichos* of the world'; do not read *halichos* but *halachos*."

***

**3.** 24 Teves is the *yahrzeit* (day of passing) of the Alter Rebbe.

24 Teves is also mentioned in the Gemara in *Bava Basra*: "On the twenty-fourth of Teves we returned to our judgment (our first trial), for the Sadducees were saying that a daughter should inherit with the daughter of the son. Rabban Yochanan ben Zakkai engaged them, etc., and defeated them, and they made that day a holiday."

However, this is a separate matter, and it has a lengthy explanation, etc., whereas the discussion now concerns us: that this day for us is the *yahrzeit* of the Alter Rebbe, and we must utilize it for everything related to walking in his ways and paths, as follows.

**4.** First and foremost—regarding the matter emphasized in his name:

Regarding the name of the Alter Rebbe—"Shneur"—it is told in the *sichos* that it hints at "two lights" (*shnei or*): "Light" refers to Torah, as it is written, "For a *mitzvah* is a candle and Torah is light." There are two categories of light: the light of the revealed Torah and the light of the teachings of Chassidus. Both together are hinted at in the name "Shneur"—one name, in which both lights are included, both the light of the revealed Torah and the light of the teachings of Chassidus.

And according to the language of the Midrash regarding the verse, "He declares His words to Jacob, His statutes and His judgments to Israel": "What He does, He tells Israel to do" (and similarly the reverse: what He commands Israel, He does)—which is also true of the *tzaddikim* who "are like their Creator," that what they do, they demand also from those who are connected and belong to them—it is understood that this is what the Alter Rebbe demands from all those who identify with and are counted among his banner and camp—after he opened and paved the way—that they too should have the unification of the light of the revealed Torah with the light of the teachings of Chassidus.

In simple terms:

The Alter Rebbe demands from each one of us both the study of the revealed Torah and the study of the teachings of Chassidus—just as he rules in the *Laws of Torah Study* regarding the obligation of every person to study Torah, that this obligation is not for a specific section of Torah, but for all sections of Torah, both the revealed Torah and the hidden Torah.

Consequently, when there are "Chassidim of the peak" who want to fulfill their obligation with this line, or conversely, "lukewarm Chassidim" who want to fulfill their obligation with that line—we say to them: *Neither this nor that*, but it must be "two lights" specifically, the two lights of the revealed Torah and the teachings of Chassidus, as they are unified together specifically.

**5.** It should be added and emphasized more specifically that just as it is impossible to be satisfied with studying the revealed Torah without the hidden Torah, so too it is impossible to be satisfied with studying the hidden Torah without the revealed Torah. For the revealed Torah and the hidden Torah are like body and soul, "the bodies of Torah" and "the soul of Torah," and just as a body is impossible without a soul, so too a soul is impossible without a body (since that is not the intent), but rather the soul of Torah together with the bodies of Torah.

It should be noted that in the *Iggeres HaKodesh* in the *Tanya*, both at its beginning and at its end, the Alter Rebbe specifically demands and emphasizes the study of the revealed Torah—the *Shas* distribution: at the beginning—"the completion of the entire *Shas* in most towns and *minyanim* of *Anash*... year after year," and at the end—"to finish the entire *Shas* every year in every city, to divide the tractates, etc."

Likewise, the Rebbe Rayatz told several stories on this matter, the content of which is that the Alter Rebbe also forcefully demanded the study of the revealed Torah.

Similarly, in the *Kuntres Eitz Chaim* by the Rebbe Rashab, he explains several details regarding the manner of studying the revealed Torah, establishing several hours a day that one must engage in the revealed Torah, just as he establishes several hours a day for the study of the teachings of Chassidus.

For this reason, they demand so forcefully from all Chassidim in general, and especially from those who are in the "tent of Torah," and from the students of the *yeshiva* in particular—that they should have extreme diligence and perseverance in the study of the revealed Torah, but that the study should be with vitality and "liveliness" (not only vitality and "liveliness" from his own understanding and grasp, but also the vitality and "liveliness" that the teachings of Chassidus add to the study of the revealed).

And the "demand" of the Rebbes is (not only a demand, but it contains) also the granting of strength, meaning that the necessary strengths were given to study the revealed and Chassidus together, in addition to the fact that the granting of strength itself obligates one to utilize the strengths to their fullest. For the lack of utilization of the strengths, *chas v'shalom*, constitutes a wrong not only toward oneself, in the sense of "one who injures himself," which, although he is not permitted, nevertheless, he is exempt, but also toward others, in the sense of "one who injures others," and all the more so when it concerns the *Nasi* of the generation!

Since this is so, it is the duty and privilege of each one to study not only the teachings of Chassidus, but also the revealed Torah (to no lesser degree), since they demand and give strength for both.

**6.** And this matter—the study of the revealed Torah with the hidden Torah, the bodies of Torah and the soul of Torah, through which a complete soul is created in a complete body—also constitutes a preparation and introduction to the complete Redemption that must be both regarding the body, the redemption of the body, and regarding the soul, the redemption of the soul.

The action of a Jew that will bring about the complete Redemption is through the completeness of his connection with Hashem, through the Torah, which is the intermediary through which the connection of Israel with Hashem is made.

And since the connection with Hashem must be in a way that the "revealed" and the "hidden" of Israel are connected with the "revealed" and the "hidden" of Hashem, there must also be his share in the Torah ("and give us our share in Your Torah," "our share" specifically) that connects Israel with Hashem, both in the "revealed" of Torah and in the "hidden" of Torah, for then the connection with Hashem is an "everlasting bond"—which is from two bonds—hidden and revealed.

And when there is an everlasting bond between Israel and Hashem, after the removal of the barrier of "your iniquities were separating between you and your God," then comes the true and complete Redemption, as the Alter Rebbe explains in *Iggeres HaTeshuvah* that immediately after the blessing of "Forgive us" we bless "Redeemer of Israel"—so may it be for us speedily in our days through Mashiach Tzidkeinu.

***

**7.** It was discussed above (in the *Hadran*) that there is a reasoning that the beginning of a time period includes "in potential" the continuation of the time that comes after it, but this reasoning is only according to the view of Beis Shammai, and only in the time to come will the *halacha* be according to them.

It should be added that even in this time we find that there is a time that includes several times, but the discussion is (not about the continuation of the time that comes after it, but the opposite) about the continuation of the time *that already was before it*.

And we find this matter regarding a *yahrzeit*—as the Alter Rebbe (the *baal ha-yahrzeit* of 24 Teves) explains in *Iggeres HaKodesh* that on the day of the *yahrzeit*, "all his deeds, his Torah, and his service that he performed all the days of his life" are elevated and included, meaning that the time of the *yahrzeit* includes within it the entire duration of a person's life.

**8.** And this elevation is not only at the time of the passing, but it is so also on the day of the *yahrzeit* every year.

As explained in the writings of the Arizal on the verse, "And these days are remembered and performed in every generation," that when they are "remembered" in a later generation, then all the revelations of the lights that shone in "these days" the first time return and are "performed."

From this it is also understood regarding the *yahrzeit* of the Alter Rebbe on 24 Teves, that through the remembrance ("remembered") of this day every year, all the elevations that were on the day of the passing return and are "performed"—in the words of the *baal ha-yahrzeit*—the elevation and inclusion of "all his deeds, his Torah, and his service that he (the Alter Rebbe) performed all the days of his life."

[It should be noted that this language—"his service that he performed"—is according to the language of the verse, "His work that He performed in it," which is said in the *Haftorah* of *Parshas Va’era*, in which week 24 Teves falls (in this year, and in most years, and) also the first time, as the passing was on "Motzaei Shabbos Kodesh Shemos, the 23rd, entering the 24th of Teves" (in the words of the Tzemach Tzedek), meaning, in the week of *Parshas Va’era*.]

It should be added that according to what the Alter Rebbe writes in *Iggeres HaTeshuvah* (regarding an undesirable matter) that "because he is the one who continues the influence to them, therefore he takes the lead" (and all the more so in the good, for "a good measure is greater, etc.")—it is understood that all those who "remember" the day of the *yahrzeit* (and especially when they accept upon themselves matters connected with the ways and paths of the *baal ha-yahrzeit*), and consequently all the elevations of the *yahrzeit* are "performed" *by them*—*they take the lead* in all the revelations that are made above, through which there is also made below an addition of light in the greatest abundance, and first and foremost—regarding us in particular—an addition of strength and the highest assistance in the service of each and every one in the ways and paths of the *baal ha-yahrzeit*.

**9.** And among the main matters in this:

The service of the Alter Rebbe was primarily—as the Rebbe Rayatz said in several *sichos*—to reveal the path of Chassidus Chabad, and not to be satisfied with that, but also to spread the path of Chassidus Chabad. And as is known, the Alter Rebbe wanted the teachings of Chassidus Chabad to belong (not only to individuals of stature, and not only to a special type of Jew, but) to every Jew.

The Alter Rebbe engaged in this himself, and our Rebbes, his successors, engaged in this, and they handed it over to us and commanded us to engage in this—in order to *bestow the merit* upon us in this.

The Rebbe Rayatz said regarding the writing of the *Sefer Torah* for the welcoming of Mashiach Tzidkeinu, that it was in his power to write the *Sefer Torah* himself, but he wanted to *bestow the merit of this upon all of Israel*, and therefore he gave the ability and the possibility that everyone could participate in this.

And so it is regarding the engagement in spreading the wellsprings outward in order to bring about the coming of Mashiach ("When will the Master come? When your wellsprings spread outward")—that our Rebbes could have brought this about themselves in the manner of "a *tzaddik* decrees and Hashem fulfills," but their desire was to *bestow the merit upon each one of us* that we too should have a connection and participation in the action of bringing Mashiach ("bringing Mashiach down") here below ten *tefachim*, and therefore they left us a place so that we too, from our side, could do something in spreading the wellsprings outward. For when we have a share in the preparation and introduction to the coming of Mashiach ("your wellsprings will spread outward"), we will also have a share—not in the manner of "bread of shame"—in the coming of Mashiach ("the Master will come"), speedily in our days.

**10.** And in this matter too, the Alter Rebbe showed the way—in what manner the action of spreading the wellsprings outward must be done:

The Rebbe Rayatz told in several *sichos* about the way the Alter Rebbe acted in spreading the wellsprings outward—that upon arriving in any city, he would enter the *beis medrash* or the *shul*, stand and say a matter in the *revealed* Torah, and after he would "capture" the audience of learners ("when he would capture the audience")—or confuse them... (in each place according to its matter)—through his words in the revealed Torah, he would begin to say a matter from the teachings of Chassidus, and thereby he would transform them from one essence to another!

And this way the Alter Rebbe showed also to us,

—and not only that, but after he opened and paved the way, our work in this is incomparably easier, and all the more so since we are promised that this work will certainly be successful—

that when we meet a Jew who for the time being is not a Chassid, we must "capture" him ("capture him") through the *revealed Torah*, and when we confuse him or break his reality through this, then he becomes a vessel for the teachings of Chassidus, and we can transform him from one essence to another.

Chassidim in general, and *Temimim* in particular, from whom the Rebbes demanded especially to be "candles to illuminate," must go and light the candles, "the candle of Hashem is the soul of man," of those Jews whose candle is not currently burning and illuminating within them, and it goes without saying that it is not illuminating outside of them—after each one precedes and lights his own candle first, and through the "fire" within him lights also the candles of other Jews. But, in order for that Jew (whose candle we must light) to allow us to approach him—we must "capture him" in the way the Alter Rebbe showed—through the revealed Torah, and through this he will certainly succeed, and afterwards he will also make him a Chassid. However, for this, one needs "weapons"... and not only the teachings of Chassidus, but also the revealed Torah.

And since each one of us has been made a "shooter" (archer)—as the expression that was common with the Rebbe Rayatz: "Each one of us has been made into a shooter"—we have no choice but to take the rifle and shoot, and presumably the shot will be toward the correct target ("one will hit where one needs to shoot").

—(The Rebbe smiled and said:) There is a story about someone who said that he knows how to shoot, but he does not know (since they forgot to teach him) how one must hold the rifle, from this side or from the other side... but regarding our matter: since "there is a presumption that a colleague does not produce from his hand something that is not fixed," then, when they gave us rifles, presumably they gave us rifles such that we can shoot only in the correct direction!

We only need to load the rifle with a "bullet" of the revealed Torah, and through this we nullify the other from his reality, until we transform him from one essence to another, as mentioned above.

**11.** And this is not coming for a sermon, but the intent is regarding actual practice:

One must add strength and extra effort in the study of the revealed Torah, for, in addition to the greatness of the matter of fulfilling a *mitzvah*, and especially the *mitzvah* of Torah study in its own right, we are also promised that the study of the revealed Torah will be a great assistance in our mission in this world—spreading the wellsprings outward—that it will give us a special approach, and a special qualification and preparation for the fulfillment of this mission, so that speedily in our days we will be able to see in every place and corner ("wherever one turns") that a "Chassid *yid*" (who walks as a Chassid) in a way that another reality does not take up place at all, like the well-known story-proverb, meaning, that in every place and in every corner ("wherever one turns and wherever one directs oneself") there will be *Elokus* in revelation, as it is written, "The earth will be full of the knowledge of Hashem."

And this matter is done through the light in the Torah, this is the teachings of Chassidus, the soul of Torah, and with the introduction of the "body" and the "vessel"—broad vessels—broad and great knowledge in the revealed Torah, each one according to his value, standing, and situation. And then the desire of the *baal ha-yahrzeit* is fulfilled that there should be the unification of the revealed and Chassidus together, and in a way that we see the action of the teachings of Chassidus also in the revealed Torah—as the Rebbe Rayatz explains in the *Kuntres Limud HaChassidus* that there are two matters in the teachings of Chassidus: (a) through it one receives knowledge in the order of *hishtalshelus* and knowledge of the Creator, (b) through it one receives a different view ("a different look"), a different understanding and grasp, and a completely different vitality in the revealed Torah. And for this (for matter b) there is a need for "material" (substances)—the revealed Torah—in which there will be the action of the teachings of Chassidus to add vitality, etc.

And in order to fulfill the desire and the will and the yearning of the *baal ha-yahrzeit*—there is a need for all those who are demanded to be "candles to illuminate" to be able to leave their four cubits and travel throughout the world and confuse and shake up the world through the *revealed* Torah, and then they will say: "See what has come," and they will sanctify them through the hidden Torah, and thereby make them "lively and happy Jews," in the two lights in the Torah, the light of the revealed and the light of the hidden.

***

**12.** It is known the story that the Rebbe Rayatz told about the order that the Maggid established with the Alter Rebbe at the beginning of his arrival in Mezritch, that his son (R' Avraham) the "Malach" would study with the Alter Rebbe the teachings of Chassidus (the teachings of the Baal Shem Tov and the Maggid), and the Alter Rebbe would study with the "Malach" the revealed Torah. And when they began to study the beginning of the *Shas*, "From what time do we recite the *Shema* in the evenings," and the Alter Rebbe began to explain the *Mishnah* according to the revealed, the "Malach" said that he explains "from what time" (*mei'eimatai*) from the language of awe and fear, etc.

And one must explain the lesson and instruction from this story, for all the stories that they told us, we can and must derive from them a lesson and instruction in matters belonging to us.

—Not long ago I received a letter from someone, and in it he writes to me that it is found in the books that stories of *tzaddikim* are in the category of "Maaseh Merkavah."

(The Rebbe smiled and said:) I have not found this proverb in the books, and therefore I leave this on the responsibility of the aforementioned... but, what is certainly clear is that in stories of *tzaddikim* there is an intent and purpose, and especially a story that one hears from a *tzaddik*, and in particular—from a *Nasi*, whose entire essence and matter is to lead his flock, when a *Nasi* tells a story (like the aforementioned story), it is certainly a matter belonging to us that we can and must learn the instruction in it, as follows.

**13.** And first and foremost—the lesson and instruction from the joining and connection of the Alter Rebbe and the Malach to study together, a joining and connection of two Jews in the study of Torah:

One Jew by himself—is not a complete reality, but rather, a reality such that he "is fit to be joined" with another Jew, who, through their joining together, there is made (not only an addition in quantity, but also) a change in quality, from one extreme to the other.

And proof of this—from that which we find in the Midrash regarding the Giving of the Torah, that "if Israel were missing even one person, etc.":

The Giving of the Torah is the most elevated matter—the purpose of the intent of the creation of all the worlds, since in the Giving of the Torah there was the nullification of the decree, the nullification of the cutting ("the cut") and the separation between the upper and lower realms ("the upper realms would not descend to the lower realms and the lower realms would not ascend to the upper realms"), and a connection was made between the upper and lower realms ("the lower realms will ascend to the upper realms and the upper realms will descend to the lower realms").

And all this depends on the joining of one Jew, whoever he may be, to the totality of Israel, for "if Israel were missing even one person, etc.," and only through the joining of that one is the totality of the matter of the Giving of the Torah made possible.

And from this it is also understood the greatness of the elevation that is made through the connection and joining of one Jew to a general matter of Torah and *mitzvos* [and all matters of Torah and *mitzvos* are general matters: regarding *mitzvos*, every *mitzvah* is included in all the *mitzvos*, as explained in the *maamarim* of Chassidus that the reason for the fact that "one who is engaged in a *mitzvah* is exempt from a *mitzvah*" is because in the *mitzvah* that he is engaged in, the *mitzvah* that he is exempt from is also included; and all the more so regarding Torah, which is above time and place, unlike *mitzvos* which have the division of time and place, like the division of the 248 limbs and 365 sinews (which are against the 248 positive *mitzvos* and 365 negative *mitzvos*), unlike Torah which is like the blood that goes and circulates in all the limbs equally]—that this is not an addition in quantity only, but a change and great elevation in quality.

**14.** And in order that the connection and joining should be *true*—this is through the introduction of "From what time do we recite the *Shema* in the evenings," "from what time" (*mei'eimatai*) from the language of awe and fear (according to the interpretation of the "Malach"), which is the matter of acceptance of the yoke:

A joining and connection—can be in a way that it is only toward the outside, while inside the status and situation of separation remains, and nevertheless it is considered a connection. And as we find in the *Mishnah* differences in laws in the manners of joining and connection regarding ritual impurity and purity. But, the intent is that there should be a joining and connection both from the outside and from the inside, etc., a true joining and connection, and even truly to the truth.

And more specifically:

When the connection of one Jew with his fellow is from ulterior motives, physical motives, or even spiritual motives—this is not a true connection, for his intent is not to the connection itself, but to the profit and benefit in the physical or spiritual as a result of the connection.

And for example: when several Jews join for a *minyan* so that they will have the merit to say a matter of holiness, knowing the greatness of the elevation that is in the matter—this is indeed a connection, and a connection in a matter of Torah and *mitzvos*, but, if the intent of the connection is only from this benefit—which is indeed a benefit in holiness, that an elevation will be added in his soul, in his spirit, and in his *nefesh*, but nevertheless only a private benefit—this is a connection from an ulterior motive, and is not a true connection, truly to the truth.

A true connection is (not from any ulterior motive and benefit, but) from the *truth of the matter*—as the Alter Rebbe explains in *Tanya* that "the *nefesh* and the *ruach*—who knows their greatness and their elevation in their root and their source in the Living God, since they are all compatible and one Father for all of them... from the side of the root of their *nefesh* in the One Hashem," and through this is made "Bless us, our Father, all of us as one in the light of Your face," "Your face" specifically, the inwardness and essence of the *Ein Sof* *Baruch Hu*.

And to add, that this connection which is "from the side of the root of their *nefesh* in the One Hashem," is above the totality of Torah and *mitzvos*, for the root of the souls of Israel is above the root of Torah and *mitzvos*, as our Sages said, "The thought of Israel preceded everything," even the thought of Torah, for in the Torah itself it is said "Command the children of Israel," "Speak to the children of Israel," and from this is proof that "Israel preceded."

And such a connection is demanded from a Jew while he is below in this physical world—in which he must complete the supreme intent to be for Him, may He be blessed, a dwelling place in the lower realms, through the revelation and continuation of the "essence of the *Shechinah*" (which was in the lower realms), above the level of the "*Shechinah*" as it is in all the worlds, even in the world of *Atzilus* and in the worlds of the *Ein Sof* that are before *Atzilus*—that also below in this physical world his connection with his fellow should be (not from the benefit that is in the matter, even a benefit of holiness, but) "from the side of the root of their *nefesh* in the One Hashem," which is above Torah and *mitzvos*.

And since this connection is above the totality of the matter of Torah and *mitzvos*, the way to come to this is through *acceptance of the yoke* specifically. And this is the hint and instruction from the continuation of the story about the study of the Alter Rebbe and the Malach, that the Malach explained "from what time" from the language of awe and fear, a matter of acceptance of the yoke, which is also the content of "we recite the *Shema* in the evenings," "he should accept upon himself the yoke of the Kingdom of Heaven first."

**15.** And this matter constitutes the beginning of the study of the Oral Torah, and in a way that the study is both in the revealed Torah and in the teachings of Chassidus (the interpretation of the Malach in the words of the *Mishnah*) together:

The matter of the Oral Torah—as the Alter Rebbe explains in *Iggeres HaKodesh* in the interpretation of the statement at the conclusion of the *Shas* "Whoever studies *halachos* every day, etc."—is that in it is revealed the Supreme Will, may He be blessed, which is clothed in the 613 *mitzvos* of the Torah, to know the action that they should do, how to conduct oneself in thought, speech, and action in actual practice in the day-to-day life in this physical world.

And the general point of His will, may He be blessed, from each one of Israel—is that the connection of Israel with Hashem should be through the Torah both in the revealed and in the hidden, as the statement "Three bonds are bound one to the other, Israel, Torah, and Hashem, and all of them are hidden and revealed,"
